<meta name="DC.Title" content="Desiccation Tolerance Evolved through Gene Duplication and Network Rewiring in Lindernia" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1105/tpc.18.00517" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2018-12-01" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="American Society of Plant Biologists" />
<meta name="DC.Rights" content="© 2018 American Society of Plant Biologists. All rights reserved.." />
<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="open-access" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="Although several resurrection plant genomes have been sequenced, the lack of suitable dehydration-sensitive outgroups has limited genomic insights into the origin of desiccation tolerance. Here, we utilized a comparative system of closely related desiccation-tolerant ( Lindernia brevidens ) and -sensitive ( Lindernia subracemosa ) species to identify gene- and pathway-level changes associated with the evolution of desiccation tolerance. The two high-quality Lindernia genomes we assembled are largely collinear, and over 90% of genes are conserved. L. brevidens and L. subracemosa have evidence of an ancient, shared whole-genome duplication event, and retained genes have neofunctionalized, with desiccation-specific expression in L. brevidens . Tandem gene duplicates also are enriched in desiccation-associated functions, including a dramatic expansion of early light-induced proteins from 4 to 26 copies in L. brevidens . A comparative differential gene coexpression analysis between L. brevidens and L. subracemosa supports extensive network rewiring across early dehydration, desiccation, and rehydration time courses. Many LATE EMBRYOGENESIS ABUNDANT genes show significantly higher expression in L. brevidens compared with their orthologs in L. subracemosa . Coexpression modules uniquely upregulated during desiccation in L. brevidens are enriched with seed-specific and abscisic acid-associated cis -regulatory elements. These modules contain a wide array of seed-associated genes that have no expression in the desiccation-sensitive L. subracemosa . Together, these findings suggest that desiccation tolerance evolved through a combination of gene duplications and network-level rewiring of existing seed desiccation pathways." />

<meta name="citation_title" content="Desiccation Tolerance Evolved through Gene Duplication and Network Rewiring in Lindernia" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Although several resurrection plant genomes have been sequenced, the lack of suitable dehydration-sensitive outgroups has limited genomic insights into the origin of desiccation tolerance. Here, we utilized a comparative system of closely related desiccation-tolerant (&lt;i&gt;Lindernia brevidens&lt;/i&gt;) and -sensitive (&lt;i&gt;Lindernia subracemosa&lt;/i&gt;) species to identify gene- and pathway-level changes associated with the evolution of desiccation tolerance. The two high-quality &lt;i&gt;Lindernia&lt;/i&gt; genomes we assembled are largely collinear, and over 90% of genes are conserved. &lt;i&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/i&gt; have evidence of an ancient, shared whole-genome duplication event, and retained genes have neofunctionalized, with desiccation-specific expression in &lt;i&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/i&gt;. Tandem gene duplicates also are enriched in desiccation-associated functions, including a dramatic expansion of early light-induced proteins from 4 to 26 copies in &lt;i&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/i&gt;. A comparative differential gene coexpression analysis between &lt;i&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/i&gt; supports extensive network rewiring across early dehydration, desiccation, and rehydration time courses. Many &lt;i&gt;LATE EMBRYOGENESIS ABUNDANT&lt;/i&gt; genes show significantly higher expression in &lt;i&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/i&gt; compared with their orthologs in &lt;i&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/i&gt;. Coexpression modules uniquely upregulated during desiccation in &lt;i&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/i&gt; are enriched with seed-specific and abscisic acid-associated &lt;i&gt;cis&lt;/i&gt;-regulatory elements. These modules contain a wide array of seed-associated genes that have no expression in the desiccation-sensitive &lt;i&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/i&gt;. Together, these findings suggest that desiccation tolerance evolved through a combination of gene duplications and network-level rewiring of existing seed desiccation pathways.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Desiccation Tolerance Evolved through Gene Duplication and Network Rewiring in Lindernia" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/30/12.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Although several resurrection plant genomes have been sequenced, the lack of suitable dehydration-sensitive outgroups has limited genomic insights into the origin of desiccation tolerance. Here, we utilized a comparative system of closely related desiccation-tolerant ( Lindernia brevidens ) and -sensitive ( Lindernia subracemosa ) species to identify gene- and pathway-level changes associated with the evolution of desiccation tolerance. The two high-quality Lindernia genomes we assembled are largely collinear, and over 90% of genes are conserved. L. brevidens and L. subracemosa have evidence of an ancient, shared whole-genome duplication event, and retained genes have neofunctionalized, with desiccation-specific expression in L. brevidens . Tandem gene duplicates also are enriched in desiccation-associated functions, including a dramatic expansion of early light-induced proteins from 4 to 26 copies in L. brevidens . A comparative differential gene coexpression analysis between L. brevidens and L. subracemosa supports extensive network rewiring across early dehydration, desiccation, and rehydration time courses. Many LATE EMBRYOGENESIS ABUNDANT genes show significantly higher expression in L. brevidens compared with their orthologs in L. subracemosa . Coexpression modules uniquely upregulated during desiccation in L. brevidens are enriched with seed-specific and abscisic acid-associated cis -regulatory elements. These modules contain a wide array of seed-associated genes that have no expression in the desiccation-sensitive L. subracemosa . Together, these findings suggest that desiccation tolerance evolved through a combination of gene duplications and network-level rewiring of existing seed desiccation pathways." />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="Although several resurrection plant genomes have been sequenced, the lack of suitable dehydration-sensitive outgroups has limited genomic insights into the origin of desiccation tolerance. Here, we utilized a comparative system of closely related desiccation-tolerant ( Lindernia brevidens ) and -sensitive ( Lindernia subracemosa ) species to identify gene- and pathway-level changes associated with the evolution of desiccation tolerance. The two high-quality Lindernia genomes we assembled are largely collinear, and over 90% of genes are conserved. L. brevidens and L. subracemosa have evidence of an ancient, shared whole-genome duplication event, and retained genes have neofunctionalized, with desiccation-specific expression in L. brevidens . Tandem gene duplicates also are enriched in desiccation-associated functions, including a dramatic expansion of early light-induced proteins from 4 to 26 copies in L. brevidens . A comparative differential gene coexpression analysis between L. brevidens and L. subracemosa supports extensive network rewiring across early dehydration, desiccation, and rehydration time courses. Many LATE EMBRYOGENESIS ABUNDANT genes show significantly higher expression in L. brevidens compared with their orthologs in L. subracemosa . Coexpression modules uniquely upregulated during desiccation in L. brevidens are enriched with seed-specific and abscisic acid-associated cis -regulatory elements. These modules contain a wide array of seed-associated genes that have no expression in the desiccation-sensitive L. subracemosa . Together, these findings suggest that desiccation tolerance evolved through a combination of gene duplications and network-level rewiring of existing seed desiccation pathways." />

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><ul class="copyright-statement"><li class="fn" id="copyright-statement-1">© 2018 American Society of Plant Biologists. All rights reserved.</li></ul><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-5">Although several resurrection plant genomes have been sequenced, the lack of suitable dehydration-sensitive outgroups has limited genomic insights into the origin of desiccation tolerance. Here, we utilized a comparative system of closely related desiccation-tolerant (<em>Lindernia brevidens</em>) and -sensitive (<em>Lindernia subracemosa</em>) species to identify gene- and pathway-level changes associated with the evolution of desiccation tolerance. The two high-quality <em>Lindernia</em> genomes we assembled are largely collinear, and over 90% of genes are conserved. <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> have evidence of an ancient, shared whole-genome duplication event, and retained genes have neofunctionalized, with desiccation-specific expression in <em>L. brevidens</em>. Tandem gene duplicates also are enriched in desiccation-associated functions, including a dramatic expansion of early light-induced proteins from 4 to 26 copies in <em>L. brevidens</em>. A comparative differential gene coexpression analysis between <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> supports extensive network rewiring across early dehydration, desiccation, and rehydration time courses. Many <em>LATE EMBRYOGENESIS ABUNDANT</em> genes show significantly higher expression in <em>L. brevidens</em> compared with their orthologs in <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Coexpression modules uniquely upregulated during desiccation in <em>L. brevidens</em> are enriched with seed-specific and abscisic acid-associated <em>cis</em>-regulatory elements. These modules contain a wide array of seed-associated genes that have no expression in the desiccation-sensitive <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Together, these findings suggest that desiccation tolerance evolved through a combination of gene duplications and network-level rewiring of existing seed desiccation pathways.</p><p id="p-6"></p></div><div class="section introduction" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">INTRODUCTION</h2><p id="p-7">Comparative systems are a powerful tool for dissecting the molecular basis of complex biological traits. The origins of desiccation tolerance in resurrection plants are largely unknown, but the underlying genetic signatures could be traced using pairs of closely related desiccation-sensitive and -tolerant species. Such an approach has been applied to <em>Eragrostis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">Vander Willigen et al., 2001</a>), <em>Selaginella</em> (<a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Yobi et al., 2013</a>), and <em>Sporobolus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">Oliver et al., 2011</a>) at the morphological and biochemical levels to identify signatures that distinguish drought and desiccation responses. Detailed pairwise comparisons have identified changes in cell wall composition (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">Plancot et al., 2014</a>), metabolite and osmoprotectant accumulation (<a id="xref-ref-45-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">Oliver et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-70-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Yobi et al., 2013</a>), and physical properties unique to desiccation-tolerant species. Although genomes are available for several resurrection plants (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">VanBuren et al., 2015</a>, <a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">2018</a>; <a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Xiao et al., 2015</a>; <a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Costa et al., 2017</a>), genomic resources in these comparative lineages are limited, and no genomes of closely related desiccation-sensitive species have been sequenced. High-quality reference genomes are available for the desiccation-sensitive <em>Selaginella moellendorffii</em> (<a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">Banks et al., 2011</a>) and the desiccation-tolerant <em>Selaginella lepidophylla</em> (<a id="xref-ref-62-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">VanBuren et al., 2018</a>), but their estimated divergence 248 million years ago prevents detailed genomic comparisons (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Baniaga et al., 2016</a>).</p><p id="p-8">Resurrection plants endure extreme and prolonged drought events through vegetative desiccation, entering a preserved and protected quiescent state that functionally mirrors seed dormancy in angiosperms. Desiccation tolerance was a critical adaptation during early land plant evolution, and many early-diverging fern, moss, and lycophyte lineages have retained or convergently evolved these ancestral resilience mechanisms (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">Proctor, 1990</a>; <a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">Oliver et al., 2000</a>; <a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">Lüttge et al., 2011</a>). Vegetative desiccation tolerance is comparatively less common in angiosperms, and recent genomic and metabolic studies suggest that it evolved through rewiring seed desiccation pathways (<a id="xref-ref-15-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Costa et al., 2017</a>; <a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">VanBuren et al., 2017</a>). Resurrection plants have a conserved set of molecular signatures associated with desiccation tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">Illing et al., 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-72-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">Zhang and Bartels, 2018</a>), but the underlying genomic basis of this trait is largely unknown. The cooption of seed- and desiccation-associated pathways in resurrection plants can occur through a broad range of mechanisms at the gene, pathway, or network level. Changes in gene regulation at network hubs could drive pathway-level rewiring to upregulate a cascade of desiccation-related mechanisms. Changes in <em>cis</em>-regulation at isolated nodes in a network could shift the stoichiometry or abundance of endpoint metabolites and proteins related to desiccation. Gene and genome duplication also can drive adaptive evolution by providing additional copies for pathway- and gene-level subfunctionalization and neofunctionalization.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="" class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-643524302" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure1" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F1.medium.gif" width="696" height="464"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure1" src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F1.medium.gif" width="696" height="464"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/72973" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div></div><p id="p-9">Desiccation tolerance is prominent in Linderniaceae (order Lamiales) within the clade spanning <em>Craterostigma</em> and <em>Lindernia</em> (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">Rahmanzadeh et al., 2005</a>). <em>Craterostigma plantagineum</em> is a model resurrection plant (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">Bartels and Salamini, 2001</a>) native to rocky outcrops of sub-Saharan Africa. All <em>Craterostigma</em> and some desert-adapted <em>Lindernia</em> species are desiccation tolerant, but most <em>Lindernia</em> species are desiccation sensitive. <em>Lindernia brevidens</em> is unusual in that it displays desiccation tolerance despite an endemic habitat in the montane rainforests of eastern Africa that never experience seasonal drying (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">Phillips et al., 2008</a>). Desiccation tolerance is likely an ancestral trait in this group (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">Fischer et al., 2013</a>) and was retained in <em>L. brevidens</em> before its radiation to the tropical rainforest. <em>Lindernia</em> is paraphyletic (<a id="xref-ref-23-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">Fischer et al., 2013</a>), and species outside of the clade containing <em>Craterostigma</em> and <em>L. brevidens</em>, such as <em>Lindernia subracemosa</em>, are desiccation sensitive. This diversity in desiccation tolerance makes <em>Lindernia</em> an excellent comparative system in which to test the contribution of gene duplication, <em>cis</em>-elements, and pathway rewiring in the evolution of desiccation tolerance. Here, we assembled high-quality reference genomes for the desiccation-tolerant <em>L. brevidens</em> and desiccation-sensitive <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Detailed comparative genomics and differential coexpression network analysis allowed us to survey the genetic basis of desiccation tolerance in <em>Lindernia</em>."</p></div><div class="section results" id="sec-2"><h2 class="">RESULTS</h2><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Comparative Grade Reference Genomes for <em>Lindernia</em></h3><p id="p-10"><em>C. plantagineum</em> is a well-studied model for the evolution of desiccation tolerance in eudicots, but its highly complex, octoploid genome has hindered genome-scale analyses. <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> are diploid with relatively small genomes (270 and 250 Mb, respectively), providing an excellent alternative system (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 1</a>).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparative Desiccation Tolerance System within Linderniaceae. Inferred phylogeny from Fischer et al. (2013) shows the two model desiccation-tolerant species (C. plantagineum and L. brevidens) and the desiccation-sensitive outgroup (L. subracemosa). Ploidy, karyotype, and genome size are shown on branches." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-643524302" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Comparative Desiccation Tolerance System within Linderniaceae. Inferred phylogeny from Fischer et al. (2013) shows the two model desiccation-tolerant species (&lt;em&gt;C. plantagineum&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt;) and the desiccation-sensitive outgroup (&lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt;). Ploidy, karyotype, and genome size are shown on branches.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="284"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 1." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="284"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/72974" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Figure 1.</span> <p id="p-11" class="first-child">Comparative Desiccation Tolerance System within Linderniaceae.</p><p id="p-12">Inferred phylogeny from <a id="xref-ref-23-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">Fischer et al. (2013)</a> shows the two model desiccation-tolerant species (<em>C. plantagineum</em> and <em>L. brevidens</em>) and the desiccation-sensitive outgroup (<em>L. subracemosa</em>). Ploidy, karyotype, and genome size are shown on branches.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-13">We generated high-quality reference genomes for both <em>Lindernia</em> species using a PacBio-based, single-molecule real-time sequencing approach. In total, we generated 21.7 and 17.9 Gb of filtered PacBio data, collectively representing 80.3× and 71.6× coverage for <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>, respectively (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Figure 1</a>). Raw PacBio reads were error corrected and assembled using the long-read assembler Canu (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Koren et al., 2017</a>), which is optimized to avoid collapsing highly repetitive and tandemly duplicated regions. Contigs were polished using high-coverage Illumina data with Pilon (<a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">Walker et al., 2014</a>) to remove residual errors. The <em>L. brevidens</em> assembly spanned 265 Mb across 267 contigs with a contig N50 (length where half or more of the assembly is contained) of 3.6 Mb. The <em>L. subracemosa</em> assembly was slightly smaller, at 246 Mb with 328 contigs and an N50 of 1.9 Mb (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>). The total assembly sizes were consistent with the estimated genome sizes of 270 and 250 Mb based on flow cytometry. <em>Lindernia</em> species are primarily self-pollinated with low residual within-genome heterozygosity, which contributed to the high contiguity and relatively simple graph-based assembly structures (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Figures 2 and 3</a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/73002/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/73002/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> <span class="caption-title"><em>Lindernia</em> Genome Assembly Metrics</span><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-18">We used high-throughput chromatin conformation capture (Hi-C) to generate a chromosome-scale assembly of <em>L. brevidens</em>. The Hi-C-based Illumina reads were mapped to the draft assembly using bwa (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">Li, 2013</a>) followed by filtering and proximity-based clustering using the Juicer pipeline (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">Durand et al., 2016</a>) (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 1</a>). This approach yielded 14 high-confidence clusters corresponding to the haploid chromosome number in <em>L. brevidens</em> (2<em>n</em> = 2<em>x</em> = 28; <a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figure 2</a>). In total, 121 contigs were ordered and oriented into 14 scaffolds collectively representing 94.7% of the assembly (249 out of 263 Mb; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 2</a>). This included anchoring 98.8% of the predicted gene models. The repetitive element density was inversely correlated with gene density, and most chromosomes contained large tracts of retrotransposons (RTs), which likely correlate with centromere position (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 3</a>).</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Hi-C Clustering for Pseudomolecule Construction in L. brevidens. A postclustering heat map of Hi-C-based intrachromosomal interactions in L. brevidens is shown. Pseudomolecules corresponding to the 14 haploid chromosomes are delineated by gray boxes." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-643524302" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Hi-C Clustering for Pseudomolecule Construction in &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt;. A postclustering heat map of Hi-C-based intrachromosomal interactions in &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; is shown. Pseudomolecules corresponding to the 14 haploid chromosomes are delineated by gray boxes.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="432"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 2." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="432"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/72991" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 2.</span> <p id="p-19" class="first-child">Hi-C Clustering for Pseudomolecule Construction in <em>L. brevidens</em>.</p><p id="p-20">A postclustering heat map of Hi-C-based intrachromosomal interactions in <em>L. brevidens</em> is shown. Pseudomolecules corresponding to the 14 haploid chromosomes are delineated by gray boxes.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Landscape of the L. brevidens Genome. LTR-RT and coding sequence (CDS) density are plotted in sliding windows of 50 kb with 25-kb step size for the 14 L. brevidens scaffolds (chromosomes). Red indicates high density and blue indicates low density of coding sequence and LTR-RTs in the heat map below each landscape." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-643524302" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Landscape of the &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; Genome. LTR-RT and coding sequence (CDS) density are plotted in sliding windows of 50 kb with 25-kb step size for the 14 &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; scaffolds (chromosomes). Red indicates high density and blue indicates low density of coding sequence and LTR-RTs in the heat map below each landscape.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F4.medium.gif" width="347" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 3." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F4.medium.gif" width="347" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F4.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/72927" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 3.</span> <p id="p-21" class="first-child">Landscape of the <em>L. brevidens</em> Genome.</p><p id="p-22">LTR-RT and coding sequence (CDS) density are plotted in sliding windows of 50 kb with 25-kb step size for the 14 <em>L. brevidens</em> scaffolds (chromosomes). Red indicates high density and blue indicates low density of coding sequence and LTR-RTs in the heat map below each landscape.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-23">The genomes of <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> were of similar size and the same karyotype, suggesting that they should have comparable repetitive elements and gene composition. Long terminal repeat (LTR)-RTs were the most abundant repetitive elements in both genomes, and they collectively spanned 34% (92.0 Mb) and 31% (77.4 Mb) of the <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> genomes, respectively (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>). Despite the similar overall LTR composition, <em>L. subracemosa</em> had significantly more intact LTRs compared with <em>L. brevidens</em> (1972 versus 1025; Wilcoxon rank sum, P &lt; 0.05). The distribution of LTR-RT insertion time was similar in both species, and most intact elements inserted within the last 1 million years (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 4</a>). These findings suggest that LTR-RTs are similarly active in both genomes but may fractionate more quickly in <em>L. brevidens</em>.</p><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Insertion Time of Intact LTR-RTs. The average insertion time calculated from the divergence of LTR pairs is plotted for the 1025 intact LTR-RTs in L. brevidens and the 1972 intact LTR-RTs in L. subracemosa. MYA, million years ago." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-643524302" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Insertion Time of Intact LTR-RTs. The average insertion time calculated from the divergence of LTR pairs is plotted for the 1025 intact LTR-RTs in &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; and the 1972 intact LTR-RTs in &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt;. MYA, million years ago.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 4." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="206"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 4." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="206"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 4." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/73008" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 4.</span> <p id="p-24" class="first-child">Insertion Time of Intact LTR-RTs.</p><p id="p-25">The average insertion time calculated from the divergence of LTR pairs is plotted for the 1025 intact LTR-RTs in <em>L. brevidens</em> and the 1972 intact LTR-RTs in <em>L. subracemosa</em>. MYA, million years ago.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-26">The overall gene composition was similar in both <em>Lindernia</em> species, though <em>L. subracemosa</em> had more annotated gene models. Ab initio gene prediction using the dehydration time-course RNAseq data and protein similarities to other angiosperms identified 27,204 and 33,344 gene models in <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa,</em> respectively (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-3" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>). We assessed annotation quality using the BUSCO pipeline and found 91% and 90% (1319 and 1298) of the 1440 genes in the Embryophyta data set present in the <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> assemblies. This proportion is comparable with results from other recent PacBio-based genomes.</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Comparative Genomics of <em>Lindernia</em></h3><p id="p-27">The <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> genomes were largely collinear based on whole-genome alignment, and 24,053 <em>L. brevidens</em> genes had syntenic orthologs in <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Roughly 70% of the genomes were conserved in 2:2 syntenic blocks, supporting a shared, ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD) event in both species (<a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Figure 5</a>; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Figures 4 and 5</a>). Six of the seven ancestral homeologous chromosome pairs from the WGD were intact in <em>L. brevidens</em>, including modern chromosome pairs: 1 and 13, 2 and 14, 3 and 5, 6 and 9, 7 and 10, and 8 and 11 (<a id="xref-fig-6-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Figure 5A</a>). Two of the ancestral homeologous chromosomes were fused in modern chromosome 5, and chromosome 12 contained fragments from several ancestral chromosomes. Chromosomal rearrangements were difficult to identify in <em>L. subracemosa</em> given its contig-level assembly, but there were no obvious rearrangements based on macrosynteny with <em>L. brevidens</em> (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Figure 5</a>). The ancestral subgenomes were heavily fractionated, and only 7742 gene pairs were retained in duplicate in <em>L. brevidens</em> and 8452 in <em>L. subracemosa</em> based on synteny. Gene-level fractionation was biased toward a dominant subgenome that contained significantly more genes (<a id="xref-fig-6-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Figure 5B</a>; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Figure 6</a>).</p><div id="F6" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparative Genomics of L. brevidens and L. subracemosa. (A) Syntenic dot plot of L. brevidens showing retained gene pairs from the recent WGD event. Each black dot represents a pair of retained genes. (B) Microsynteny between syntenic blocks of the L. brevidens (top) and L. subracemosa (bottom) genomes. Syntenic gene pairs between L. brevidens versus L. subracemosa are shown by brown connections, and retained WGD gene pairs within each genome are shown by gray connections. Genes are colored by orientation in L. brevidens (light blue are forward, dark blue are reverse) and L. subracemosa (light red are forward, dark red are reverse)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-643524302" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Comparative Genomics of &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; Syntenic dot plot of &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; showing retained gene pairs from the recent WGD event. Each black dot represents a pair of retained genes. &lt;strong&gt;(B)&lt;/strong&gt; Microsynteny between syntenic blocks of the &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; (top) and &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt; (bottom) genomes. Syntenic gene pairs between &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; versus &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt; are shown by brown connections, and retained WGD gene pairs within each genome are shown by gray connections. Genes are colored by orientation in &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; (light blue are forward, dark blue are reverse) and &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt; (light red are forward, dark red are reverse).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 5." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F6.medium.gif" width="285" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 5." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F6.medium.gif" width="285" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 5." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F6.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/72926" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 5.</span> <p id="p-28" class="first-child">Comparative Genomics of <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>.</p><p id="p-29"><strong>(A)</strong> Syntenic dot plot of <em>L. brevidens</em> showing retained gene pairs from the recent WGD event. Each black dot represents a pair of retained genes.</p><p id="p-30"><strong>(B)</strong> Microsynteny between syntenic blocks of the <em>L. brevidens</em> (top) and <em>L. subracemosa</em> (bottom) genomes. Syntenic gene pairs between <em>L. brevidens</em> versus <em>L. subracemosa</em> are shown by brown connections, and retained WGD gene pairs within each genome are shown by gray connections. Genes are colored by orientation in <em>L. brevidens</em> (light blue are forward, dark blue are reverse) and <em>L. subracemosa</em> (light red are forward, dark red are reverse).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-31">We identified patterns of gene duplication and loss that may be related to the evolution of desiccation tolerance and other lineage-specific traits. Most gene pairs from the WGD were either retained in duplicate or fractionated to single copies in both species, including 11,874 single-copy genes (1:1) and 7568 duplicated genes (2:2) in both genomes (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>). We identified 3200 lineage-specific genes in <em>L. brevidens</em> (1:0 or 2:0) and 7067 lineage-specific genes in <em>L. subracemosa</em> (0:1 or 0:2) based on synteny. The higher number of lineage-specific genes in <em>L. subracemosa</em> was likely related to differences in total annotated gene number (27,204 versus 33,344). The lineage-specific genes in L. <em>brevidens</em> were enriched in Gene Ontology (GO) terms related to chlorophyll biosynthesis and metabolism, regulation of mitosis, and response to heat, which may suggest a role for these pathways in desiccation tolerance (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 3</a>).</p><div id="T2" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/72914/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/72914/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 2.</span> <span class="caption-title">Comparison of Biased Fractionation Following the Shared WGD in <em>Lindernia</em></span><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-33">New genes can arise through tandem gene duplication, and tandem gene duplications are associated with adaptive evolution (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">Cannon et al., 2004</a>), including of desiccation tolerance in other resurrection plants (<a id="xref-ref-60-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">VanBuren et al., 2015</a>, <a id="xref-ref-62-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">2018</a>). <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> had a similar overall number of tandem genes but major differences in array size. <em>L. brevidens</em> had 2673 tandem arrays containing 5345 genes with array sizes ranging from 2 to 24 members. <em>L. subracemosa</em> had 3404 tandem arrays across 6809 genes with array sizes ranging from 2 to 31 (<a id="xref-fig-7-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Figure 6</a>). Through cross-referencing with syntenic gene pairs, we found that most tandem arrays were conserved between <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Only 153 tandem arrays were specific to <em>L. brevidens</em> and 247 arrays were specific to <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Although tandem gene arrays were generally conserved, array sizes were highly variable and few contained the same number of genes between species (<a id="xref-fig-7-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Figure 6B</a>). Together, these data suggest that most tandem gene duplication events are ancestral but that each species has undergone unique array expansion and contraction.</p><div id="F7" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F7.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparison of Tandem Gene Arrays in Lindernia. (A) Histogram of tandem array sizes. (B) Heat map of tandem array size in syntenic orthologs between L. brevidens and L. subracemosa. Values are plotted as the proportion of tandem genes in each category against all the genes in that array size." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-643524302" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Comparison of Tandem Gene Arrays in &lt;em&gt;Lindernia&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; Histogram of tandem array sizes. &lt;strong&gt;(B)&lt;/strong&gt; Heat map of tandem array size in syntenic orthologs between &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt;. Values are plotted as the proportion of tandem genes in each category against all the genes in that array size.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 6." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F7.medium.gif" width="309" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 6." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F7.medium.gif" width="309" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F7.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 6." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F7.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/72953" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 6.</span> <p id="p-34" class="first-child">Comparison of Tandem Gene Arrays in <em>Lindernia</em>.</p><p id="p-35"><strong>(A)</strong> Histogram of tandem array sizes.</p><p id="p-36"><strong>(B)</strong> Heat map of tandem array size in syntenic orthologs between <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Values are plotted as the proportion of tandem genes in each category against all the genes in that array size.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Global Expression Patterns and Desiccation-Related Network Rewiring</h3><p id="p-37">To construct a comparative framework of genes related to desiccation, we conducted parallel sampling of leaf tissue during desiccation and rehydration time courses in <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Parallel sampling between species allowed us to distinguish between genes involved in typical dehydration responses and those related specifically to desiccation tolerance. Sampling ranged from mild dehydration stress (relative water content [RWC] 53–56%; 3 d) through severe dehydration (RWC 23–27%; 7 d) and desiccation (RWC 6–9%; 10 and 14 d), followed by 24 and 48 h post rehydration (<a id="xref-fig-8-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F8">Figure 7</a>). RWC was 53 to 56% at day 3 and fell below 10% after 10 d of drought in both species (<a id="xref-fig-8-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F8">Figure 7A</a>). <em>L. subracemosa</em> plants were largely dead upon rehydration, and <em>L. brevidens</em> plants were mostly viable and physiologically active at 48 h post rehydration (RWC 44%) (<a id="xref-fig-8-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F8">Figure 7</a>).</p><div id="F8" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F8.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Overview of Desiccation and Rehydration Processes in Lindernia. (A) RWC of fresh leaf tissues (F), 3, 7, 10, and 14 d of drought (D), and 24 and 48 h post rehydration (R) in L. brevidens and L. subracemosa. Error bars represent the se with three replicates for each RWC measurement. (B) Representative L. brevidens (top) and L. subracemosa (bottom) at various RWC." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-643524302" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Overview of Desiccation and Rehydration Processes in &lt;em&gt;Lindernia&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; RWC of fresh leaf tissues (F), 3, 7, 10, and 14 d of drought (D), and 24 and 48 h post rehydration (R) in &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt;. Error bars represent the se with three replicates for each RWC measurement. &lt;strong&gt;(B)&lt;/strong&gt; Representative &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; (top) and &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt; (bottom) at various RWC.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 7." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F8.medium.gif" width="307" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 7." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F8.medium.gif" width="307" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F8.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 7." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F8.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/72902" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 7.</span> <p id="p-38" class="first-child">Overview of Desiccation and Rehydration Processes in <em>Lindernia</em>.</p><p id="p-39"><strong>(A)</strong> RWC of fresh leaf tissues (F), 3, 7, 10, and 14 d of drought (D), and 24 and 48 h post rehydration (R) in <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Error bars represent the <span class="sc">se</span> with three replicates for each RWC measurement.</p><p id="p-40"><strong>(B)</strong> Representative <em>L. brevidens</em> (top) and <em>L. subracemosa</em> (bottom) at various RWC.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-41">The greatest changes in gene expression occurred at two time points during the transition from mild to severe dehydration stress and from desiccated to rehydrated (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 4</a>). The number of differentially expressed genes between well-watered and mild dehydration (F versus D3) were relatively similar in both species (5322 versus 4824 in <em>L. subracemosa</em> and <em>L. brevidens</em>). Many syntenic gene pairs had similar expression levels, with 581 upregulated and 133 downregulated in both species. Significantly more genes were differentially expressed between mild and severe dehydration stress (D3 versus D7), with 4329 and 9227 differentially expressed genes, respectively. A similar proportion of syntenic gene pairs was upregulated in both species at D7 (581), but significantly more gene pairs were similarly downregulated (1396) compared with mild dehydration stress. This pattern suggests that there is conservation of downregulated pathways in desiccation-sensitive and -tolerant species.</p><p id="p-42">In <em>L. brevidens</em>, gene expression was relatively stable from severe dehydration to desiccation (D7, D10, and D14), whereas a high proportion of genes were differentially expressed in <em>L. subracemosa</em> during the transition to desiccation. This reflects the stability of desiccated <em>L. brevidens</em> and the imminent death of <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Few genes were similarly differentially expressed in both species under severe dehydration and desiccation (D7 versus D10: 50 and 62; D10 versus D14: 3 and 0, upregulated and downregulated, respectively; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 4</a>). A substantial proportion of syntenic gene pairs (2065) were similarly upregulated in both species during early rehydration (24 h), supporting the conclusion that there is conserved activation of repair pathways. Expression changes in both species were minimal between 24 and 48 h post rehydration. Although <em>L. subracemosa</em> had some transcriptional response post rehydration, this was not sufficient to repair the extensive desiccation-induced damage. Together, the divergent expression patterns suggest that there is extensive upregulation of distinct pathways with desiccation-specific roles.</p><p id="p-43">We conducted GO enrichment analysis of the gene pairs that were uniquely upregulated in <em>L. brevidens</em>, with no change or a decrease in expression in <em>L. subracemosa</em>. We reasoned that such genes are likely to be specific to the induction of desiccation tolerance. Most of the GO terms enriched among genes upregulated in mild dehydration stress (D3) were related to responses to abiotic stress and secondary metabolite biosynthesis (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 5</a>), suggesting early activation of protective mechanisms. There were only a few GO terms enriched among genes upregulated in severe dehydration (D7) and desiccation (D10 and D14), including terms related to transport, vacuole organization, ion homeostasis, and RNA modification (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 5</a>). Most GO terms of genes uniquely downregulated in <em>L. brevidens</em> under mild and severe dehydration stress were related to photosynthesis processes, suggesting that the photosynthetic apparatus is inactivated early under mild dehydration compared with <em>L. subracemosa</em> (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 6</a>).</p><p id="p-44">The large-scale expression changes unique to desiccation in <em>L. brevidens</em> may be driven by changes in <em>cis</em>-regulation. Genes with unique desiccation-related expression in <em>L. brevidens</em> were enriched with <em>cis</em>-regulatory elements associated with dehydration and abscisic acid (ABA)-mediated responses as well as seed development pathways (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 7</a>). Enriched <em>cis-</em>elements associated with typical ABA-mediated dehydration responses included ABF1 and ABF2 (<a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71">Yoshida et al., 2015</a>) among others. Enriched seed maturation-associated <em>cis-</em>elements included bZIP53 (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">Alonso et al., 2009</a>), ABA-responsive element binding protein3 (AREB3; <a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">Nakashima et al., 2009</a>), and ABI5 (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">Lopez-Molina et al., 2001</a>). <em>Cis</em>-elements at the interface of heat- and dehydration-mediated ABA responses, such as Heat Stress Factor A6b (HSFA6B; <a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">Huang et al., 2016</a>) and HSF7, also were enriched in desiccation-related genes. Enriched <em>cis</em>-elements in genes downregulated during desiccation in <em>L. brevidens</em> had wide roles in plant growth and development, hormone responses, and photosynthesis (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 8</a>).</p><p id="p-45">To compare network-level gene expression in <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>, we utilized a weighted correlation network analysis approach (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">Langfelder and Horvath, 2008</a>) across the dehydration and rehydration RNAseq time courses. This comparative coexpression network approach allowed us to parse conserved dehydration-related patterns from desiccation-specific pathway rewiring. After filtering genes with low expression (see Methods), we constructed two coexpression networks, with 14,246 genes in 10 modules for <em>L. brevidens</em> and 14,075 genes in 9 modules for <em>L. subracemosa</em> (<a id="xref-fig-9-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Figure 8</a>; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Figures 7 and 8</a>). Based on their temporal dynamics, coexpression modules could be broadly classified into three groups: (1) high expression in well-watered tissue but downregulation in dehydration/desiccation; (2) expression during early dehydration; and (3) sustained high expression throughout dehydration and desiccation (<a id="xref-fig-9-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Figure 8</a>). Modules 1, 2, 3, and 5 in the <em>L. brevidens</em> network and modules 2, 4, and 5 in <em>L. subracemosa</em> had high expression in well-watered and rehydrating conditions, with decreasing expression throughout dehydration/desiccation time points (<a id="xref-fig-9-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Figure 8</a>). Modules 7 and 8 in <em>L. brevidens</em> and modules 3, 7, and 8 in <em>L. subracemosa</em> were involved in early dehydration responses, with peak expression at 3 or 7 d of dehydration. Modules 4, 6, and 10 in <em>L. brevidens</em> and modules 1 and 3 in <em>L. subracemosa</em> had sustained dehydration and desiccation-induced expression.</p><div id="F9" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F9.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparative Coexpression Networks during Desiccation and Rehydration in Lindernia. The mean expression of genes from modules in the coexpression network in L. brevidens (left) and L. subracemosa (right) are plotted for the seven time points. Conserved modules with upregulation during desiccation are highlighted in yellow in both networks, and modules with downregulation during desiccation are highlighted in gray." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-643524302" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Comparative Coexpression Networks during Desiccation and Rehydration in &lt;em&gt;Lindernia&lt;/em&gt;. The mean expression of genes from modules in the coexpression network in &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; (left) and &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt; (right) are plotted for the seven time points. Conserved modules with upregulation during desiccation are highlighted in yellow in both networks, and modules with downregulation during desiccation are highlighted in gray.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 8." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F9.medium.gif" width="440" height="190"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 8." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F9.medium.gif" width="440" height="190"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F9.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 8." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F9.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/72965" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 8.</span> <p id="p-46" class="first-child">Comparative Coexpression Networks during Desiccation and Rehydration in <em>Lindernia</em>.</p><p id="p-47">The mean expression of genes from modules in the coexpression network in <em>L. brevidens</em> (left) and <em>L. subracemosa</em> (right) are plotted for the seven time points. Conserved modules with upregulation during desiccation are highlighted in yellow in both networks, and modules with downregulation during desiccation are highlighted in gray.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-48">We compared module overlap between the networks to identify patterns of conservation and species-specific divergence. Modules downregulated during severe dehydration and desiccation were largely conserved between the two species, with 74% of gene pairs falling in the same group of modules (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Figure 9</a>). Modules upregulated during dehydration and desiccation had comparatively little overlap between species, and only 43% of gene pairs were in overlapping modules (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Figure 9</a>). This indicates that there was significant pathway rewiring during desiccation. Network-wide <em>cis</em>-regulatory element enrichment patterns mirrored the observations comparing pairwise differentially expressed genes. Desiccation-associated modules from the <em>L. brevidens</em> network were enriched in dehydration-associated ABA-responsive <em>cis</em>-elements and seed maturation-associated <em>cis</em>-elements, including bZIP53, AREB3, and ABI5 among others (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 9</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Unique Desiccation-Related Pathways in <em>L. brevidens</em></h3><p id="p-49">The similarities between seed and vegetative desiccation suggest overlapping pathways, which is supported by expression data from several resurrection plant lineages (<a id="xref-ref-15-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Costa et al., 2017</a>; <a id="xref-ref-61-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">VanBuren et al., 2017</a>). We identified a wide range of seed-specific genes and pathways that were expressed only under dehydration in <em>L. brevidens</em> compared with syntenic orthologs in <em>L. subracemosa</em> (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 10</a>). Seed storage proteins serve as a reserve of nitrogen, carbon, and sulfur for germinating seeds, and they likely play a role in seed longevity (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">Nguyen et al., 2015</a>). Orthologs to genes encoding 2S and 12S seed storage proteins were generally upregulated in <em>L. brevidens</em> under desiccation, and syntenic orthologs in <em>L. subracemosa</em> were not expressed or were expressed highly in well-watered conditions (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 10</a>). Delay of germination1 (DOG1) is an essential component of seed dormancy regulation, and its expression affects hundreds of seed-related genes (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">Dekkers et al., 2016</a>). <em>DOG1</em> was highly expressed in well-watered <em>L. brevidens</em> tissues but was downregulated during desiccation. The <em>L. subracemosa DOG1</em> transcript had a low basal-level expression in all time points.</p><p id="p-50">Oil bodies are lipid organelles filled with triacylglycerols that function as high-density energy reserves during seed germination. Oil bodies accumulate in desiccated leaf tissue of <em>Oropetium</em> and likely play a role in desiccation tolerance. Oil bodies are enveloped with oleosin structural proteins that prevent membrane coalescence and protect membrane integrity during freeze-thaw cycles (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Shimada et al., 2008</a>). Oil body membranes also are studded with calcium binding caleosin proteins that are associated with oil body degradation (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Poxleitner et al., 2006</a>) and general stress response pathways (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">Shen et al., 2014</a>). <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> had similar numbers of genes for oleosin (eight versus seven) and caleosin (four versus three) proteins, although <em>L. brevidens</em> had more retained whole-genome and tandem duplicates (<a id="xref-fig-10-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F10">Figure 9</a>). Most oleosin and caleosin genes had low or undetectable expression in well-watered tissue, but several were induced during progressive dehydration and desiccation. Six oleosin genes in <em>L. brevidens</em> and three oleosin genes in <em>L. subracemosa</em> were upregulated in desiccating tissue, with most having a peak expression of less than 30 transcripts per million (TPM). <em>L. brevidens</em> had a pair of retained duplicated oleosin genes where one ortholog peaked at more than 500 TPMs in desiccating tissue, and the single syntenic ortholog in <em>L. subracemosa</em> had a relatively low expression. No caleosin genes were upregulated in <em>L. subracemosa</em> upon desiccation, but a pair of syntenic orthologs in <em>L. brevidens</em> were abundantly expressed (<a id="xref-fig-10-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F10">Figure 9</a>).</p><div id="F10" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F10.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Subfunctionalization of Oleosin and Caleosin Genes in L. brevidens. Heat maps of log2 transformed expression of genes encoding oleosins (top) and caleosins (bottom) in L. brevidens and L. subracemosa. Syntenic orthologs are connected by brown lines including 1:2 and 2:2 orthologs between species." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-643524302" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Subfunctionalization of Oleosin and Caleosin Genes in &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt;. Heat maps of log&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; transformed expression of genes encoding oleosins (top) and caleosins (bottom) in &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt;. Syntenic orthologs are connected by brown lines including 1:2 and 2:2 orthologs between species.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 9." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F10.medium.gif" width="361" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 9." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F10.medium.gif" width="361" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F10.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 9." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F10.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/72992" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 9.</span> <p id="p-51" class="first-child">Subfunctionalization of Oleosin and Caleosin Genes in <em>L. brevidens</em>.</p><p id="p-52">Heat maps of log<sub>2</sub> transformed expression of genes encoding oleosins (top) and caleosins (bottom) in <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Syntenic orthologs are connected by brown lines including 1:2 and 2:2 orthologs between species.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-53">Early light-induced proteins (ELIPs) are predicted to bind chlorophyll and function in photoprotection under high light and other abiotic stresses. The <em>L. subracemosa</em> genome had four genes encoding ELIP proteins, including a pair of syntelogs retained from the WGD event (<a id="xref-fig-11-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F11">Figure 10</a>). Expression of two <em>ELIP</em> genes was hardly detectable during the surveyed time points, and two others were highly expressed during dehydration. The number of ELIPs in <em>L. subracemosa</em> was similar to that in other desiccation-sensitive angiosperms, and their dehydration-induced expression was consistent with the hypothesized protective mechanisms (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">Hayami et al., 2015</a>). By contrast, the <em>L. brevidens</em> genome had undergone a dramatic expansion of ELIP genes, with 26 in total, including a large tandem array of 19 duplicates. This large tandem array was collinear to a pair of retained syntenic orthologs in <em>L. subracemosa</em> and a single retained gene copy in <em>L. brevidens</em>. Nearly all the <em>ELIP</em> genes in this array, and dispersed copies throughout the genome, were highly expressed during severe dehydration, desiccation, and rehydration, but they were hardly expressed in well-watered and mildly dehydrated tissue (<a id="xref-fig-11-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F11">Figure 10B</a>). The tandem array was syntenic with the highly expressed ortholog in <em>L. subracemosa</em>, and the single-copy syntelog in <em>L. brevidens</em> and its syntenic ortholog in <em>L. subracemosa</em> were not expressed in dehydrated tissue. This suggests an ancestral subfunctionalization of this duplicated pair where only one gene copy was involved in dehydration-related responses. After the divergence of <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>, the dehydration-specific syntelog likely underwent massive tandem proliferation in <em>L. brevidens</em>.</p><div id="F11" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F11.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Tandem Proliferation of Desiccation-Associated ELIP Genes in L. brevidens. (A) Microsynteny of a large tandem gene array in L. brevidens compared with the single-gene syntenic ortholog in L. brevidens and the two syntenic regions in L. subracemosa. (B) Log2 transformed expression patterns of ELIPs in L. subracemosa (top) and L. brevidens (bottom). The large tandem array and syntenic whole-genome duplicates are labeled." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-643524302" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Tandem Proliferation of Desiccation-Associated &lt;em&gt;ELIP&lt;/em&gt; Genes in &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; Microsynteny of a large tandem gene array in &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; compared with the single-gene syntenic ortholog in &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; and the two syntenic regions in &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;strong&gt;(B)&lt;/strong&gt; Log&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; transformed expression patterns of &lt;em&gt;ELIP&lt;/em&gt;s in &lt;em&gt;L. subracemosa&lt;/em&gt; (top) and &lt;em&gt;L. brevidens&lt;/em&gt; (bottom). The large tandem array and syntenic whole-genome duplicates are labeled.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 10." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F11.medium.gif" width="440" height="370"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 10." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F11.medium.gif" width="440" height="370"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F11.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 10." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/30/12/2943/F11.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/72921" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 10.</span> <p id="p-54" class="first-child">Tandem Proliferation of Desiccation-Associated <em>ELIP</em> Genes in <em>L. brevidens</em>.</p><p id="p-55"><strong>(A)</strong> Microsynteny of a large tandem gene array in <em>L. brevidens</em> compared with the single-gene syntenic ortholog in <em>L. brevidens</em> and the two syntenic regions in <em>L. subracemosa</em>.</p><p id="p-56"><strong>(B)</strong> Log<sub>2</sub> transformed expression patterns of <em>ELIP</em>s in <em>L. subracemosa</em> (top) and <em>L. brevidens</em> (bottom). The large tandem array and syntenic whole-genome duplicates are labeled.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-57">In Arabidopsis (<em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>), STAY-GREEN (SGR) proteins are key regulators of chlorophyll degradation, and they are typically upregulated under abiotic stresses (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">Sakuraba et al., 2014b</a>). Syntenic orthologs of SGR were highly expressed in both <em>Lindernia</em> species during dehydration/desiccation.
