STAY-GREEN LIKE (SGRL) proteins are negative regulators of chlorophyll degradation, and overexpression of SGRL2 leads to a stay-green phenotype (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">Sakuraba et al., 2014a</a>). The syntenic ortholog of SGRL was highly induced during desiccation in <em>L. brevidens</em>, but the <em>L. subracemosa</em> ortholog had no detectable expression (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 10</a>).</p><p id="p-58">Carbohydrate metabolism is heavily shifted during desiccation, and sucrose, trehalose, and short-chain oligosaccharides function as osmoprotectants to stabilize cellular macromolecules. Sucrose is the most abundant carbohydrate in most resurrection plants, and the accumulation of sucrose distinguishes desiccation-sensitive and -tolerant <em>Eragrostis</em> species (<a id="xref-ref-31-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">Illing et al., 2005</a>). <em>Craterostigma</em> and <em>L. brevidens</em> accumulate the unusual C8 sugar 2-octulose in photosynthetic tissues, which serves as a reservoir of sucrose accumulation during desiccation (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">Bianchi et al., 1991</a>; <a id="xref-ref-48-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">Phillips et al., 2008</a>). Transketolase7 and -10 catalyze the formation of octulose-8-phosphate in <em>Craterostigma</em> (<a id="xref-ref-74-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">Zhang et al., 2016</a>), and the orthologous transketolase genes were highly expressed in <em>L. brevidens</em> leaf tissue (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 10</a>). This included two pairs of syntenic 1:1 orthologs and a trio of retained 2:1 duplicates, with upregulation in well-watered and rehydrating tissue in <em>L. brevidens</em> and no or little expression in <em>L. subracemosa</em>.</p><p id="p-59">LATE EMBRYOGENESIS ABUNDANT (LEA) proteins are predicted to have protective functions that are essential for desiccation tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Hoekstra et al., 2001</a>; <a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">Goyal et al., 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">Hundertmark and Hincha, 2008</a>). We identified 77 and 82 LEA protein-encoding genes in <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>, respectively (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 11</a>). Orthologs were assigned for 70 of these genes. About half of the identified LEAs were classified to the LEA_2 group, whereas the second largest LEA group was the LEA_4 group (14 and 12 genes in <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>, respectively). Nine LEA genes in <em>L. brevidens</em> (one Dhn, five LEA_2, two LEA_4, and one LEA_5) were derived from gene duplication events, which suggests that these genes may have functions related to desiccation tolerance in <em>L. brevidens</em>. For example, <em>LEA5-2</em> occurs in one copy in <em>L. subracemosa</em> (<em>LsLEA5-2</em>) and has two orthologs in <em>L. brevidens</em> (i.e., <em>LbLEA5-2</em> and <em>LbLEA5-3</em>). The <em>LsLEA5-2</em> gene showed negligible expression in <em>L. subracemosa</em> under control and dehydration conditions, whereas <em>LbLEA5-2</em> was among the highest expressed <em>LEA</em> genes upon dehydration in <em>L. brevidens</em> (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Data Set </a>).</p><p id="p-60">Almost one-third of <em>L. brevidens LEA</em> genes showed expression levels 30 times higher or more than <em>L. subracemosa</em> orthologs during late dehydration (10 and 14 d) (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Data Set</a>). Most <em>L. subracemosa</em> orthologs had reduced or no expression in all surveyed time points, suggesting that there was a massive rewiring of expression networks (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Data Set </a>). For example, <em>LEA1-3</em>, <em>LEA1-4</em>, <em>LEA2-14</em>, <em>LEA2-19</em>, <em>LEA4-1</em>, <em>LEA4-6</em>, <em>LEA4-7</em>, and <em>LEA5-2</em> showed very high expression in fully hydrated <em>L. brevidens</em> but no expression in fully hydrated <em>L. subracemosa</em>.</p></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-7"><h2 class="">DISCUSSION</h2><p id="p-61">Genomic resources are abundant for resurrection plants, but the lack of suitable dehydration-sensitive outgroups has limited genomic insights into the origin and pathways controlling desiccation tolerance. Here, we leveraged a unique comparative system of closely related desiccation-tolerant and -sensitive species to identify gene- and pathway-level changes associated with the evolution of desiccation tolerance. This approach allowed us to distinguish dehydration pathways conserved in all plants from desiccation-specific processes observed only in resurrection plants.</p><p id="p-62"><em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> have similar overall genome size and gene number, and most genes were likewise retained as singletons (1:1) or duplicates (2:2) after their shared WGD event. The genomes have no significant differences in architecture, rRNAs, repetitive element composition, or clustering of desiccation-related genes. These features were proposed previously to contribute to desiccation tolerance in other resurrection plant lineages (<a id="xref-ref-68-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Xiao et al., 2015</a>; <a id="xref-ref-15-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Costa et al., 2017</a>). Instead, our data indicate that desiccation tolerance in <em>L. brevidens</em> is driven by a complex cascade of <em>cis</em>-regulatory element-mediated pathway rewiring, tandem duplication, and preferential gene retention and neofunctionalization.</p><p id="p-63">Gene expression patterns are dramatically divergent in dehydration and rehydration time-course data between <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Only a few of the syntenic orthologs are similarly expressed in both species, and coexpression network modules are largely rewired. Early dehydration responses have surprisingly little overlap, suggesting that the gene expression program and signals leading to tolerance are already apparent upon mild dehydration. Gene expression is most dynamic between the day-3 and -7 time points, when the plants shift from moderate to severe dehydration stress. This likely reflects major shifts in leaf water potential, photosynthesis, oxidative stress, and cellular damage. Gene expression is stabilized after moderate drought and desiccation in <em>L. brevidens</em>, which reflects the successful deployment of protective mechanisms. By contrast, the dynamic and chaotic expression patterns in desiccating <em>L. subracemosa</em> may reflect last-ditch efforts to avoid imminent senescence.</p><p id="p-64">Drought and seed development are linked by the common stress of water deficit. Vegetative and seed desiccation processes are strikingly similar, and overlapping pathways have been identified in resurrection plants (<a id="xref-ref-15-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Costa et al., 2017</a>; <a id="xref-ref-61-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">VanBuren et al., 2017</a>). These include accumulation of osmoprotectants, expression of LEA proteins, and free radical scavenging systems as well as downregulation of photosynthesis and dismantling the photosynthetic apparatus. Drought responses and seed development are similarly regulated by ABA-related signaling, and both elicit comparable downstream responses (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">Nakashima and Yamaguchi-Shinozaki, 2013</a>). Several important transcription factors involved in dehydration and seed-related processes are preferentially retained in <em>L. brevidens</em> compared with <em>L. subracemosa</em>, which may allow high-level pathway rewiring. Desiccation-related genes such as <em>ELIP</em>s or <em>LEA</em>s have increased in copy number in <em>L. brevidens</em> via tandem gene duplication. For genes encoding proteins with structural, enzymatic, or chaperone functions, tandem duplications may serve to increase their absolute abundance to surpass a threshold required for desiccation tolerance. The expansion of <em>ELIP</em>s has been observed in several resurrection plants, including <em>C. plantagineum</em> (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">Bartels et al., 1992</a>), <em>S. lepidophylla</em> (<a id="xref-ref-62-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">VanBuren et al., 2018</a>), and <em>Boea hygrometrica</em> (<a id="xref-ref-68-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Xiao et al., 2015</a>). ELIPs likely play an important role in protecting the photosynthetic apparatus and bind excess chlorophyll during prolonged desiccation (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">Alamillo and Bartels, 2001</a>). The repeated duplication of <em>ELIP</em>s may be a hallmark of the convergent evolution of desiccation tolerance across land plants. Expression patterns also can be shifted by changes in <em>cis-</em>regulatory elements, as was observed previously in <em>LEA</em> genes from <em>C. plantagineum</em> and <em>L. brevidens</em> (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">van den Dries et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">Giarola et al., 2018</a>). The enrichment of seed-related <em>cis-</em>regulatory elements in modules uniquely upregulated in <em>L. brevidens</em> is likely the result of novel <em>cis-</em>element acquisition in desiccation-related genes and activation of seed-related transcription factors.</p><p id="p-65">Desiccation tolerance likely evolved from a complex, additive series of gene duplications and pathway rewiring rather than a simple master regulatory switch. Naturally drought-tolerant species could undergo favorable duplication of <em>ELIP</em>s or rewiring of LEA proteins to promote a quasi-desiccation-tolerant state. These responses could be refined further through the accumulation of additive features to surpass the threshold required for surviving anhydrobiosis. This step-wise hypothesis is supported by the continuum of desiccation tolerance, where the magnitude and duration of tolerance varies across species. <em>Craterostigma</em> can tolerate more rapid desiccation and recover more completely than <em>L. brevidens</em>, and older leaf tissue in <em>L. brevidens</em> is often desiccation sensitive.</p><p id="p-66">The recovery rate in <em>L. brevidens</em> is related to environmental factors, including developmental stage, rate of drying, and dehydration priming. This comparatively weak desiccation tolerance may reflect relaxed selection in the drought-free rainforest habitat of <em>L. brevidens</em>. Desiccation tolerance is ancestral in the clade spanning <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>Craterostigma</em> (<a id="xref-ref-23-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">Fischer et al., 2013</a>), and some protective mechanisms were likely present in the shared ancestor with <em>L. subracemosa</em>. This also may explain the partial induction of seed and vegetative desiccation-associated pathways in <em>L. subracemosa</em> compared with the typical dehydration responses in other species. The trajectory from sensitive to desiccation tolerant is a complex, multistep process, and future work in intermediate or weakly desiccation-tolerant species will help uncover the origins of this trait.</p></div><div class="section methods" id="sec-8"><h2 class="">METHODS</h2><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Growth Conditions and Sampling</h3><p id="p-67"><em>Lindernia brevidens</em> and <em>Lindernia subracemosa</em> were grown as described previously (<a id="xref-ref-48-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">Phillips et al., 2008</a>). Voucher specimens have been deposited: <em>L. brevidens</em> Kenya, Taita Hills, E. Fischer 8022 (KOBL;=Herbarium Koblenz) and <em>L. subracemosa</em> Rwanda, Uwinka, E. Fischer 1350 (BG-Bonn 19990-2, KOBL). Plants were propagated via cuttings and maintained under day/night temperatures of 22 and 18°C, respectively, under fluorescent lighting with an intensity of 80 μE m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> and a 16/8-h photoperiod. <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> were grown in the same chamber to minimize environmental variance. For the desiccation and rehydration time courses, plants were allowed to gradually dry for a period of 30 d, with sampling in triplicate with three independent plants at D3, D7, D14, D21, and D30. Plants were rehydrated and sampled at 24 and 48 h post rehydration. Samples were always taken at the same time of the day, 6 h after the onset of light, to minimize effects associated with circadian oscillation. Leaf tissue for RNAseq was flash frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at −80°C. RWC measurements were calculated using the equation RWC = [(FW − DW)/(SW − DW)], where FW, DW, and SW indicate fresh weight of the leaf tissue, dry weight, and saturated weight. Dry weight was obtained after drying tissue at 80°C for 48 h, and saturated plant weights were obtained after submerging leaf tissue in water for 24 h. Three replicates of RWC measurements were collected for each time point.</p></div><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>Nucleic Acid Extraction, Library Construction, and Sequencing</h3><p id="p-68">High molecular weight genomic DNA for PacBio and Illumina sequencing was isolated from young leaf tissue of growth chamber-grown <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>. DNA was isolated using a modified nucleus preparation (<a id="xref-ref-73-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">Zhang et al., 1995</a>) followed by phenol chloroform purification to remove residual contaminants. PacBio libraries were constructed and size selected for 25-kb fragments on the BluePippen system (Sage Science) followed by purification using AMPure XP beads (Beckman Coulter). Libraries were sequenced on a Sequel platform with V4 software and V2 chemistry. In total, 2,054,566 filtered subreads spanning 21.7 Gb were sequenced for <em>L. brevidens</em> and 1,615,065 reads spanning 17.9 Gb were sequenced for <em>L. subracemosa</em>. This represents 80.3× and 71.6× coverage for <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>, respectively. Illumina DNAseq libraries for error correction were constructed using the KAPA HyperPrep Kit (Kapa Biosystems) following the manufacturer’s instructions. Libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq4000 device under paired-end 150-bp mode.</p><p id="p-69">For RNAseq analysis, total RNA was extracted from 200 mg of ground <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> leaf tissues using an Omega Bio-tek E.Z.N.A. Plant RNA Kit (Omega Bio-tek) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. RNA quality was validated using gel electrophoresis and Qubit RNA IQ Assay (ThermoFisher). Two micrograms of total RNA was used to construct Illumina TruSeq-stranded mRNA libraries following the manufacturer’s protocol (Illumina). Libraries were pooled and sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq4000 device under paired-end 150-bp mode. Three replicatfinnes were sequenced for each time point in each species.</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Assembly</h3><p id="p-70">Genome sizes for <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> were estimated using flow cytometry as described previously (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">Arumuganathan and Earle, 1991</a>). The flow cytometry-based estimates of 270 and 250 Mb for <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>, respectively, were consistent with k-mer-based analysis using Illumina WGS data. <em>Lindernia</em> are mostly selfing, and unimodal k-mer distribution suggests low within-genome heterozygosity for both species. Raw PacBio reads were error corrected and assembled using Canu (V1.4; <a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Koren et al., 2017</a>). Based on previous experience (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">Edger et al., 2018</a>; <a id="xref-ref-62-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">VanBuren et al., 2018</a>), Canu produced the most contiguous and accurate assembly for homozygous, diploid species compared with other leading long-read assemblers. The following Canu parameters were modified and all others were left as default: minReadLength = 2500, GenomeSize = 270 Mb (or 250 Mb), minOverlapLength = 1000. Assembly graphs were visualized in Bandage (<a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">Wick et al., 2015</a>). Draft contigs were polished with Pilon (V1.22; <a id="xref-ref-65-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">Walker et al., 2014</a>) using 79× and 58× coverage of Illumina paired-end 150-bp data for <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>, respectively. Illumina reads were quality trimmed using Trimmomatic (V0.33; <a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Bolger et al., 2014</a>) and aligned to the draft contigs using bowtie2 (V2.3.0; <a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">Langmead and Salzberg, 2012</a>) with default parameters. Alignment rates in the first round of corrections were 96 and 97%, respectively, suggesting that both the <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> assemblies were largely complete. Parameters for Pilon were modified as follows: –flank 7, –K 49, and –mindepth 20. Pilon was run recursively three times with minimal corrections in the third round, supporting accurate insertion/deletion correction.</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Hi-C Library Construction and Analysis</h3><p id="p-71">The <em>L. brevidens</em> draft genome was anchored into a chromosome-scale assembly using a Hi-C proximity-based assembly approach. The Hi-C library was constructed using 0.2 g of young leaf tissue from well-watered <em>L. brevidens</em> plants with the Proximo Hi-C Plant kit (Phase Genomics) following the manufacturer’s protocol. The final library was size selected for 300 to 600 bp and sequenced on the Illumina NexSeq 500 device under paired-end 75-bp mode. In total, 178 million reads were used as input for the Juicer and 3d-DNA Hi-C analysis and scaffolding pipelines (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.18.00517/DC1">Supplemental Table 1</a>; <a id="xref-ref-19-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">Durand et al., 2016</a>; <a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">Dudchenko et al., 2017</a>). Quality-filtered reads were aligned to the PacBio contigs using bwa (V0.7.16; <a id="xref-ref-37-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">Li, 2013</a>) with strict parameters (-n 0) to prevent mismatches and nonspecific alignments, and the resulting sam files were used as input into the Juicer pipeline. Read pairs were merged and duplicates or near duplicates were removed prior to constructing the distance matrix. Contigs were ordered and oriented and assembly errors were identified using the 3d-DNA pipeline with default parameters (<a id="xref-ref-18-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">Dudchenko et al., 2017</a>). The resulting hic contact matrix was visualized using Juicebox, and misassemblies and misjoins were manually corrected based on neighboring interactions. This approach identified 14 high-confidence clusters representing the haploid chromosome number in <em>L. brevidens</em>. The manually validated assembly was used to build pseudomolecules using the finalize-output.sh script from 3d-DNA, and chromosomes were renamed and ordered by size.</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Annotation</h3><p id="p-72">Prior to genome annotation, LTR-RTs were predicted using LTR harvest (genome tools V1.5.8; <a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">Ellinghaus et al., 2008</a>) and LTR finder (V1.07; <a id="xref-ref-69-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Xu and Wang, 2007</a>), and the LTR library was refined using LTR retriever (V1.8.0; <a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">Ou and Jiang, 2018</a>). RTs were classified as intact if they were flanked by full-length LTRs. The insertion time for each intact element was calculated using LTR retriever with the formula T = K/2μ, where K is the divergence rate approximated by percentage identity and μ is the neutral mutation rate estimated as μ = 1 × 10<sup>−8</sup> mutations per bp per year.</p><p id="p-73">The filtered, nonredundant LTR library from LTR retriever was used as input for whole-genome annotation of LTR-RTs using RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>; <a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">Chen, 2004</a>). The <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> genomes were annotated using the MAKER-P pipeline (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">Campbell et al., 2014</a>). Transcript-based evidence for gene predictions was produced using the desiccation/rehydration time-course RNAseq data. RNAseq reads were aligned to the <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> genomes using the splice aware aligner STAR (V2.6; <a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Dobin et al., 2013</a>). Transcripts were identified using StringTie (V1.3.4; <a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Pertea et al., 2015</a>) with default parameters, and –merge flag was used to combine the output from individual libraries. The sets of nonredundant transcripts were used as EST evidence, and protein sequences from Arabidopsis (<em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>; <a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">Lamesch et al., 2012</a>) and UniprotKB plant databases (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Boutet et al., 2007</a>) were used as protein evidence. The custom LTR-RT library produced by LTR retriever and Repbase libraries were used for repeat masking. Ab initio gene prediction was done using SNAP (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">Korf, 2004</a>) and Augustus (3.0.2; <a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">Stanke and Waack, 2003</a>), with two rounds of iterative training for each species. The raw gene models were filtered to identify any residual repetitive elements using BLAST with a nonredundant transposase library. After filtering, a final set of 27,204 and 33,344 gene models was produced for <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>, respectively. Annotation quality was assessed using BUSCO (V.2; <a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">Simão et al., 2015</a>) with the plant-specific data set (embryophyta_odb9).</p></div><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>Comparative Genomics</h3><p id="p-74">Syntenic gene pairs within and between <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> were identified using the MCScan toolkit (V1.1; <a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">Wang et al., 2012</a>) implemented in python [<a href="https://github.com/tanghaibao/jcvi/wiki/MCscan-(Python-version)">https://github.com/tanghaibao/jcvi/wiki/MCscan-(Python-version)</a>]. Gene models were aligned using LAST, and hits were filtered to find syntenic blocks. Tandem gene duplicates were identified using all-versus-all BLAST with a minimum e-value of 1e-5 and maximum gene distance of 10 genes. Macrosynteny and microsynteny plots and syntenic block depths were plotted using the python version of MCScan. Genes were classified as lineage specific if they had no syntenic orthologs between the two species or hits from LASTAL with greater than 70% nucleotide identity. The WGD event within <em>Lindernia</em> was identified using a combination of synteny and synonymous substitution rate estimation between duplicated gene pairs. Comparison of <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> in MCScan identified a 2:2 syntenic pattern with 7742 and 8452 duplicated gene pairs retained, respectively. Duplicated regions span 70% of the <em>L. brevidens</em> genome and 72% of the <em>L. subracemosa</em> genome. Most duplicated regions were retained in large blocks, allowing chromosome pairs to be identified. Substitution rate was estimated using KaKs_calculator with the NG model (<a id="xref-ref-75-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-75">Zhang et al., 2006</a>), and peaks of 0.65 in <em>L. brevidens</em> and 0.69 for <em>L. subracemosa</em> were identified, which indicates that the event is shared between both species.</p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>RNAseq Analysis</h3><p id="p-75">Paired-end Illumina RNAseq reads were trimmed by quality score and by adapter contamination using Trimmomatic (V0.33; <a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Bolger et al., 2014</a>) with default parameters. The expression level of each gene was quantified using the pseudoaligner Kallisto (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">Bray et al., 2016</a>) against the final gene models for <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em>. Parameters were left as default with 100 bootstraps per sample. Expression was quantified in TPM, and a mean across the three replicates was used for single-gene analysis and to construct log<sub>2</sub> transformed expression-based heat maps. Pairwise differentially expressed genes were identified using sleuth (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">Pimentel et al., 2017</a>) implemented in R.</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Coexpression Network Construction</h3><p id="p-76">The time-course RNAseq data were clustered into gene coexpression networks using the R package WGCNA (<a id="xref-ref-35-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">Langfelder and Horvath, 2008</a>). Genes with less than an average TPM of 5 across all seven time points were filtered prior to network construction. A signed coexpression network was constructed for each species using a soft-thresholding power of 8 and a tree cut height of 0.15. All remaining parameters were left as default. In total, 14,246 genes were clustered into 10 modules for <em>L. brevidens</em> and 14,075 genes were clustered into 9 modules for <em>L. subracemosa</em>.</p></div><div id="sec-17" class="subsection"><h3><em>Cis</em>-Element Identification</h3><p id="p-77"><em>Cis</em>-regulatory elements were identified using the Hypergeometric Optimization of Motif EnRichment program (V4.10; <a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">Heinz et al., 2010</a>) using <em>cis</em>-elements from 529 plant transcription factors (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">O’Malley et al., 2016</a>). <em>Cis</em>-elements were identified in the 1-kb region upstream of the transcriptional start site when known or directly upstream of the start codon of each gene model. Promoters of gene models with detectable expression (TPM &gt; 1) were used as background. Enrichment tests were performed using syntenic gene pairs with differential expression specific to <em>L. brevidens</em> or gene models unique to modules upregulated or downregulated during desiccation in <em>L. brevidens</em>. P &lt; 0.00001 was used as the cutoff for identifying enriched motifs in any comparison.</p></div><div id="sec-18" class="subsection"><h3>Identification of <em>LEA</em> Genes</h3><p id="p-78"><em>LEA</em> genes were retrieved from <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> transcriptomes by BLAST and HMMER (<a href="http://hmmer.org/">http://hmmer.org/</a>). Arabidopsis and <em>Craterostigma plantagineum</em> LEA protein sequences were used for BLAST searches. HMM profiles for the eight LEA families (DHN, PF00257; LEA_1, PF03760; LEA_2, PF03168; LEA_3, PF03242; LEA_4, PF02987; LEA_5, PF00477; LEA_6, PF10714; and SMP, PF04927) obtained from the Pfam database (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org">http://pfam.xfam.org</a>; <a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">Finn et al., 2016</a>) were used with the program hmmscan to search for LEA domain-containing proteins. Proteins identified with hmmscan were queried against the nr databank to confirm their classification as LEAs. Ortholog pairs were additionally confirmed by pairwise sequence alignments of the predicted protein sequences using EMBOSS Needle (<a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/psa/emboss_needle/">https://www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/psa/emboss_needle/</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-19" class="subsection"><h3>Accession Numbers</h3><p id="p-79">The genome assemblies, raw PacBio data, Illumina DNAseq, and RNAseq data are available from the National Center for Biotechnology Information Short Read Archive. The RNAseq reads were deposited to the National Center for Biotechnology Information Short Read Archive under BioProject PRJNA488068. The genome assemblies for <em>L. brevidens</em> and <em>L. subracemosa</em> were deposited under BioProjects PRJNA489464 and PRJNA489465, respectively.</p></div><div id="sec-20"
